# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

**Target Repositories (version-pinned mirrors):**

- `github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--v1.32.0` — use `repo:^github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--v1.32.0$` filter
- `github.com/sg-evals/api--v0.32.0` — use `repo:^github.com/sg-evals/api--v0.32.0$` filter

Scope ALL keyword_search/nls_search queries to these repos.
Use the repo name as the `repo` parameter for read_file/go_to_definition/find_references.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

---

**Sourcegraph Repositories:** `github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--v1.32.0`, `github.com/sg-evals/api--v0.32.0`

# Kubernetes Controller Runtime Reconciler Registration

## Your Task

Trace how a controller registers itself with the controller-runtime Manager in kubernetes/kubernetes. Find the Go source files that define the Reconciler interface, implement SetupWithManager, and wire the controller into the manager's reconcile loop.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the crossrepo tracing domain.

## Available Resources

The local `/workspace/` directory contains: sg-evals/kubernetes--v1.32.0, sg-evals/client-go--v0.32.0, sg-evals/api--v0.32.0, sg-evals/etcd-io-etcd.

**Note:** Additional repositories are accessible via Sourcegraph MCP tools:
- `sg-evals/etcd-io-etcd` (etcd-io/etcd)

75-assistanttext-
Perfect! I have completed my analysis and created the answer file. Here's a summary of my findings:

## Summary

I've traced through the kubernetes/kubernetes v1.32.0 repository to understand controller registration patterns. The key finding is:

**The `sigs.k8s.io/controller-runtime` framework is NOT part of the kubernetes/kubernetes repository.** It's a separate project maintained independently.

### What I Found Instead

The kubernetes repository itself uses a traditional workqueue-based controller pattern, exemplified by:

1. **`staging/src/k8s.io/sample-controller/controller.go`** - Shows the standard Kubernetes controller implementation:
   - Uses informers for resource watching
   - Implements a workqueue for queueing work items
   - Has a `Run()` method that starts workers
   - Uses `syncHandler()` for reconciliation logic

2. **`staging/src/k8s.io/controller-manager/controller/interfaces.go`** - Defines a minimal controller interface (not controller-runtime's Manager)

### Key Differences

The Kubernetes repository's controller pattern is fundamentally different from controller-runtime:
- **K8s native**: Direct constructors, informers, and workqueues
- **Controller-runtime**: Uses Reconciler interface and Manager pattern with `SetupWithManager()`

The answer.json file has been created at `/workspace/answer.json` with complete details about the findings, including file paths, symbols, the call chain, and detailed narrative explanation.

We also must remember to call Forget\n210: \t// if we do not want this work item being re-queued. For example, we do\n211: \t// not call Forget if a transient error occurs, instead the item is\n212: \t// put back on the workqueue and attempted again after a back-off\n213: \t// period.\n214: \tdefer c.workqueue.Done(objRef)\n215: \n216: \t// Run the syncHandler, passing it the structured reference to the object to be synced.\n217: \terr := c.syncHandler(ctx, objRef)\n218: \tif err == nil {\n219: \t\t// If no error occurs then we Forget this item so it does not\n220: \t\t// get queued again until another change happens.\n221: \t\tc.workqueue.Forget(objRef)\n222: \t\tlogger.Info(\"Successfully synced\", \"objectName\", objRef)\n223: \t\treturn true\n224: \t}\n225: \t// there was a failure so be sure to report it.  This method allows for\n226: \t// pluggable error handling which can be used for things like\n227: \t// cluster-monitoring.\n228: \tutilruntime.HandleErrorWithContext(ctx, err, \"Error syncing; requeuing for later retry\", \"objectReference\", objRef)\n229: \t// since we failed, we should requeue the item to work on later.  This\n230: \t// method will add a backoff to avoid hotlooping on particular items\n231: \t// (they're probably still not going to work right away) and overall\n232: \t// controller protection (everything I've done is broken, this controller\n233: \t// needs to calm down or it can starve other useful work) cases.\n234: \tc.workqueue.AddRateLimited(objRef)\n235: \treturn true\n236: }\n237: \n238: // syncHandler compares the actual state with the desired, and attempts to\n239: // converge the two. It then updates the Status block of the Foo resource\n240: // with the current status of the resource.\n241: func (c *Controller) syncHandler(ctx context.Context, objectRef cache.ObjectName) error {\n242: \tlogger := klog.LoggerWithValues(klog.FromContext(ctx), \"objectRef\", objectRef)\n243: \n244: \t// Get the Foo resource with this namespace/name\n245: \tfoo, err := c.foosLister.Foos(objectRef.Namespace).Get(objectRef.Name)\n246: \tif err != nil {\n247: \t\t// The Foo resource may no longer exist, in which case we stop\n248: \t\t// processing.\n249: \t\tif errors.IsNotFound(err) {\n250: \t\t\tutilruntime.HandleErrorWithContext(ctx, err, \"Foo referenced by item in work queue no longer exists\", \"objectReference\", objectRef)\n251: \t\t\treturn nil\n252: \t\t}\n253: \n254: \t\treturn err\n255: \t}\n256: \n257: \tdeploymentName := foo.Spec.DeploymentName\n258: \tif deploymentName == \"\" {\n259: \t\t// We choose to absorb the error here as the worker would requeue the\n260: \t\t// resource otherwise. This could have been caused by a\n275: \t// temporary network failure, or any other transient reason.\n276: \tif err != nil {\n277: \t\treturn err\n278: \t}\n279: \n280: \t// If the Deployment is not controlled by this Foo resource, we should log\n281: \t// a warning to the event recorder and return error msg.\n282: \tif !metav1.IsControlledBy(deployment, foo) {\n283: \t\tmsg := fmt.Sprintf(MessageResourceExists, deployment.Name)\n284: \t\tc.recorder.Event(foo, corev1.EventTypeWarning, ErrResourceExists, msg)\n285: \t\treturn fmt.Errorf(\"%s\", msg)\n286: \t}\n287: \n288: \t// If this number of the replicas on the Foo resource is specified, and the\n289: \t// number does not equal the current desired replicas on the Deployment, we\n290: \t// should update the Deployment resource.\n291: \tif foo.Spec.Replicas != nil && *foo.Spec.Replicas != *deployment.Spec.Replicas {\n292: \t\tlogger.V(4).Info(\"Update deployment resource\", \"currentReplicas\", *foo.Spec.Replicas, \"desiredReplicas\", *deployment.Spec.Replicas)\n293: \t\tdeployment, err = c.kubeclientset.AppsV1().Deployments(foo.Namespace).Update(ctx, newDeployment(foo), metav1.UpdateOptions{FieldManager: FieldManager})\n294: \t}\n295: \n296: \t// If an error occurs during Update, we'll requeue the item so we can\n297: \t// attempt processing again later. This could have been caused by a\n298: \t// temporary network failure, or any other transient reason.\n299: \tif err != nil {\n300: \t\treturn err\n301: \t}\n302: \n303: \t// Finally, we update the status block of the Foo resource to reflect the\n304: \t// current state of the world\n305: \terr = c.updateFooStatus(ctx, foo, deployment)\n306: \tif err != nil {\n307: \t\treturn err\n308: \t}\n309: \n310: \tc.recorder.Event(foo, corev1.EventTypeNormal, SuccessSynced, MessageResourceSynced)\n311: \treturn nil\n312: }\n313: \n314: func (c *Controller) updateFooStatus(ctx context.Context, foo *samplev1alpha1.Foo, deployment *appsv1.Deployment) error {\n315: \t// NEVER modify objects from the store. It's a read-only, local cache.\n316: \t// You can use DeepCopy() to make a deep copy of original object and modify this copy\n317: \t// Or create a copy manually for better performance\n318: \tfooCopy := foo.DeepCopy()\n319: \tfooCopy.Status.AvailableReplicas = deployment.Status.AvailableReplicas\n320: \t// If the CustomResourceSubresources feature gate is not enabled,\n321: \t// we must use Update instead of UpdateStatus to update the Status block of the Foo resource.\n322: \t// UpdateStatus will not allow changes to the Spec of the resource,\n323: \t// which is ideal for ensuring nothing other than resource status has been updated.\n324: \t_, err := c.sampleclientset.SamplecontrollerV1alpha1().Foos(foo.Namespace).UpdateStatus(ctx, fooCopy, metav1.UpdateOptions{FieldManager: FieldManager})\n325: \treturn err\n326: }\n327: \n328: // enqueueFoo takes a Foo resource and converts it into a namespace/name\n329: // string which is then put onto the work queue. This method should *not* be\n330: // passed resources of any type other than Foo.\n331: func (c *Controller) enqueueFoo(obj interface{}) {\n332: \tif objectRef, err := cache.ObjectToName(obj); err != nil {\n333: \t\tutilruntime.HandleError(err)\n334: \t\treturn\n335: \t} else {\n336: \t\tc.workqueue.Add(objectRef)\n337: \t}\n338: }\n339: \n340: // handleObject will take any resource implementing metav1.Object and attempt\n341: // to find the Foo resource that 'owns' it. The controller-runtime is a separate project maintained independently at https://github.com/kubernetes-sigs/controller-runtime and provides frameworks for building Kubernetes operators using a Reconciler interface and Manager pattern.\\n\\n## What Was Found\\n\\n### 1. Traditional Kubernetes Controller Pattern\\n\\nThe kubernetes repository uses a traditional workqueue-based controller pattern, as demonstrated by the sample-controller in `staging/src/k8s.io/sample-controller/`:\\n\\n- **File**: `staging/src/k8s.io/sample-controller/main.go`\\n  - Entry point that sets up the controller\\n  - Initializes client-go informers and clientsets\\n  - Creates and runs the controller with `controller.Run(ctx, workers)`\\n\\n- **File**: `staging/src/k8s.io/sample-controller/controller.go`\\n  - Defines the `Controller` struct that implements the reconciliation loop\\n  - Uses workqueue for queueing work items\\n  - Implements `Run()` method that starts workers and processes items\\n  - Uses informers and listers for caching and efficient watching\\n  - Pattern: controller watches resources via informers \u2192 enqueues changes to workqueue \u2192 workers process items and call `syncHandler`\\n\\n### 2. Controller Manager Interface\\n\\n- **File**: `staging/src/k8s.io/controller-manager/controller/interfaces.go`\\n  - Defines `Interface` with `Name() string` method\\n  - Not the same as controller-runtime's Manager interface\\n  - Defines optional `Debuggable` and `HealthCheckable` interfaces\\n\\n## Registration Pattern in kubernetes/kubernetes\\n\\nControllers in the Kubernetes repository are registered through:\\n\\n1. **Factory/Constructor**: `NewController()` creates and configures the controller\\n2. **Informer Setup**: Event handlers are registered on informers to watch resources\\n3. **Direct Execution**: `controller.Run()` is called directly from main, not through a Manager\\n4. **Workqueue**: Internal workqueue handles queueing and dequeuing of work items\\n5. **Reconciliation Loop**: `syncHandler()` processes each work item and reconciles state\\n\\nThis is fundamentally different from the controller-runtime pattern which uses:\\n- A `Reconciler` interface with `Reconcile(context.Context, reconcile.Request) (reconcile.Result, error)`\\n- A `Manager` that handles registration and lifecycle\\n- A `SetupWithManager()` method pattern for controller setup\\n\\n## Conclusion\\n\\nThe controller-runtime framework is external to the Kubernetes repository. The Kubernetes project itself uses its own controller implementation pattern based on client-go informers and workqueues, which predates and differs from the controller-runtime abstraction.\"\n}\nEOF\ncat /workspace/answer.json\n",

  "text": "# Controller Registration Pattern in Kubernetes v1.32.0\n\n## Key Finding\n\nThe `sigs.k8s.io/controller-runtime` package is **NOT included** in the kubernetes/kubernetes v1.32.0 repository. The controller-runtime is a separate project maintained independently at https://github.com/kubernetes-sigs/controller-runtime and provides frameworks for building Kubernetes operators using a Reconciler interface and Manager pattern.\n\n## What Was Found\n\n### 1. Traditional Kubernetes Controller Pattern\n\nThe kubernetes repository uses a traditional workqueue-based controller pattern, as demonstrated by the sample-controller in `staging/src/k8s.io/sample-controller/`:\n\n- **File**: `staging/src/k8s.io/sample-controller/main.go`\n  - Entry point that sets up the controller\n  - Initializes client-go informers and clientsets\n  - Creates and runs the controller with `controller.Run(ctx, workers)`\n\n- **File**: `staging/src/k8s.io/sample-controller/controller.go`\n  - Defines the `Controller` struct that implements the reconciliation loop\n  - Uses workqueue for queueing work items\n  - Implements `Run()` method that starts workers and processes items\n  - Uses informers and listers for caching and efficient watching\n  - Pattern: controller watches resources via informers → enqueues changes to workqueue → workers process items and call `syncHandler`\n\n### 2. Controller Manager Interface\n\n- **File**: `staging/src/k8s.io/controller-manager/controller/interfaces.go`\n  - Defines `Interface` with `Name() string` method\n  - Not the same as controller-runtime's Manager interface\n  - Defines optional `Debuggable` and `HealthCheckable` interfaces\n\n## Registration Pattern in kubernetes/kubernetes\n\nControllers in the Kubernetes repository are registered through:\n\n1. **Factory/Constructor**: `NewController()` creates and configures the controller\n2. **Informer Setup**: Event handlers are registered on informers to watch resources\n3. **Direct Execution**: `controller.Run()` is called directly from main, not through a Manager\n4. **Workqueue**: Internal workqueue handles queueing and dequeuing of work items\n5. **Reconciliation Loop**: `syncHandler()` processes each work item and reconciles state\n\nThis is fundamentally different from the controller-runtime pattern which uses:\n- A `Reconciler` interface with `Reconcile(context.Context, reconcile.Request) (reconcile.Result, error)`\n- A `Manager` that handles registration and lifecycle\n- A `SetupWithManager()` method pattern for controller setup\n\n## Conclusion\n\nThe controller-runtime framework is external to the Kubernetes repository. The Kubernetes project itself uses its own controller implementation pattern based on client-go informers and workqueues, which predates and differs from the controller-runtime abstraction."
